006112429X|4.0|I picked this book up after reading a review of the film by the same name starring Tilda Swinton.  Although \\"We Need to Talk About Kevin\\" is a little on the longer side (400 pages), author Lionel Shriver uses every page to tell the story of Kevin.  This book took a little warming up to, but by the end, I was wishing there was more to read.    \\"We Need to Talk About Kevin\\" tells the story of a school shooting from the eyes of an unexpected character: the perpetrator's mother.  Through a series of letters to her ex-husband, Eva Khatchadourian takes us into the mind of her sociopath son.  The story takes place two years after the momentous 'Thursday' (as Eva refers to it) and gives a window into this mother's life, now consumed with both grief and guilt over her son's actions.  Eva follows the timeline of her son Kevin's childhood, detailing the events of his life from birth that led up to his horrific crime.    I'll admit, when I selected this book, the topic intrigued me, but I was a little worried that it would be melodramatic and cliche.  This book surprised me.  Albeit the author's style takes a little warming up to (she uses a lot of big words and her style can be a little flowery), but Lionel Shriver writes in a way that really brings the audience into her story.  I could feel her frustration, sorrow, grief, etc, throughout the book.  Her style is at once haunting and endearing, at once extremely detailed and pleasantly vague, only giving details here and there about Thursday.  Like I said, it took me some time to warm up to this book, but by the end it had me in tears (a rare accomplishment for a book).  Shriver is a gifted story teller, developing the story bit by bit and building to an extremely powerful ending.  \\"We Need to Talk About Kevin\\" definitely gripped me; I'm looking forward to seeing the film.

B000F1IQN2|5.0|`Munich' (2006) is a powerful, intense, sobering and complex examination into the unseen world of international espionage and political revenge. If you enter into viewing this film with a pre-conceived worldview or mindset that is wired to accept only clearly defined categories such as black and white, right and wrong, good and evil and them and us, fasten your seatbelt and prepare for a paradigm shift.<br /><br />The film begins with the massacre of the Israeli athletic squad at the 1972 Olympic Games in Munich, Germany by the PLO and moves on from that heart wrenching historical event to weave a fictional tale of Israel's response through a convert scheme to hunt down and assassinate those responsible. At first it seems self evident as to which side is justified in their endeavor and which side deserve no reprieve from their impending punishment.<br /><br />However as the web of intrigue deepens, ethics tested and unavoidable compromises made the line between the righteous and the unrighteous slowly begins to blur and ultimately disappear. Matters are further complicated by the discovery of other covert groups, political and non political, with agendas that may hamper the Israeli game plan.<br /><br />The viewer will have much to consider by the end of the film. All the traditional questions such as \\"those who live by the sword, die by the sword', or \\"violence begets violence\\" come to mind as well as new ones that maybe you haven't considered. Emotionally and mentally drained by the time the credits roll by one is ultimately one is left to wonder if there is any answer to the eternal cycle of hatred and violence at all.

The process of cellular breakdown makes it unrecognizable. Other people call it the imagination. I think of it as a compost heap. Every so often I take an idea, plant it in the compost and wait. It feeds on that black stuff used to be a life, takes its energy for its own. It germinates. Takes root. produces shoots. And so on and so forth until one fine day I have a story or a novel.\\"<br /><br />And with that both the reader and Margaret become intrigued with Vida Winter and want to hear what she has to say if it is the truth or not.<br /><br />Setterfield pays homage to other gothic tales whose ideas she has let germinate in her own compost heap, Jane Eyre, Villette, The Woman in White, Middlemarch, among others. 0761536434|4.0|This book is exhaustive and detailed, as other reviewers have pointed out. You could hardly ask for it to have more content. However, it is frequently repetitious. I understand that the story of video games is about several different stories going on at once, so it's often necessary for the narration to go back and forth between previous years, and the \\"current\\" year in the book. However, this is often done in a way that seems to assume that you haven't read any of the other chapters that have come before. It feels like your grandfather is telling you this story, looping around and going on diversions to the point where you sometimes wonder if he's going to get back to what he started telling you about a minute ago.    Kent also drops in large block quotations from his interviews, rather than work the information into the text in a more seamless manner, so in order to be clear about what he's writing, he frequently has to repeat something that was said in the quotation. He also uses block quotations that are from other written works (e.g., magazines and newspapers) that are offset in a way that closely resembles his main text, instead of resembling the interview quotations. The source for these is given in an endnote, so if you want to see who said it and when, you have to flip all the way to the end of the book. (I also have some typographical issues with the book, but those are obviously on a more nerdy level that few people care about.)    Overall, with some editing work, this book could stand to be at least a hundred pages shorter, and be much more readable in the process. B003VBVQKS|5.0|Band<br />Come Taste The Band; the tenth Deep Purple studio album (and sole release from the short-lived MK4 line up) is truly underrated and misunderstood, although it thankfully seems to have seen something of a critical reappraisal nowadays, thanks in part to this amazing 35th anniversary edition, in addition to growing appreciation for the late guitarist Tommy Bolin.<br /><br />Right from the off, Come Taste The Band is big, loud and impressive. Opener `Commin' Home' bursts out of the speakers with pure rock fury, adding back into the band's sound some of the harder and faster rock edge that had been absent from their previous album Stormbringer (which at times can seem like its just 50% Soul/Funk and 50% Quiet Songs/Ballads.)<br /><br />Come Taste The Band definitely has power and force, but it also has variety. The funk/soul influences that the MK3 band had started using are there in tracks, but there are also more rock orientated numbers, and a few musically exciting pieces like `The Drifter,' which stray into altogether different territory. `Owed To G' even has a little of the classical vibe which the band used to play with quite a bit.<br /><br />One of the standout moments on the album is `Gettin' Tighter,' which has an absolutely gigantic chorus, which is so energetic and filled with attitude that it puts a huge grin on my face every time I listen to it. It also contains a brake-down of solid funk in the middle, which some fans may object to, but that brake-down is in a very rocking song and this is part of the success of this album, a fusion of the new and the old styles in a dramatic and joyous fashion.<br /><br />Though for the longest time (until '93), Come Taste The Band was the only Deep Purple album without Ritchie Blackmore on guitar, you really should not let that put you off, in Tommy Bolin the band found a perfect musical fit. Blackmore's absence may cause you some concern, however remember that Paice and Lord are still there, the album still has that trademark Purple sound in the undercurrent behind all the funk and soul. Paice's unique drum fill style is still there, and Lord's amazing keys abilities are present and correct.<br /><br />If you liked Burn and especially if you liked Stormbringer, then Come Taste The Band is a must listen experience... the effective Coverdale/Hughes partnership is as great as it has ever been, and the general songwriting is absolutely top of the line. I really recommend this album... just don't buy it if you are expecting it to sound like Smoke On The Water, understand what it is first, then jump right in.<br /><br />** If you get the 35th Anniversary edition, there are 3 bonus tracks (a single edit, a b-side and a jam) a very detailed set of linear notes, and a second disc containing an impressive remixing job, which unearths some new music within the songs and changes the fade outs, track order and emphasis of the mix. **e undercurrent behind all the funk and soul. 0920668372|1.0|My first review of this book was admittedly harsh, and attracted multiple venomous personal attacks from fans of this book.  So I withdrew my original review and am posting what is hopefully a calmer, more rational negative review of this book.  I dislike this book for two primary reasons:    (1)  The images more so than the words imply some sort of unhealthy parent-child relationship.  I don't understand why the mother has to surreptitiously slither across the floor to check on her child in the middle of the night.  Why doesn't she just walk in?  I also don't understand why she feels the need to drive across town with a ladder, climb into the house of her adult son, and rock him to sleep.  Although this may have been intended as humor or an allegory, to me, there is a disturbing undertone of clinginess and need.  There is attachment parenting, which can be healthy, but there is nothing healthy about rocking a grown man to sleep.  In addition, this is a CHILDREN'S book, so it is easy for a child to view this content and associated images literally.  Save the humor and metaphors for ADULT fiction.     (2)  The part of the book that really infuriated me was the implied \\"quid pro quo\\" relationship between this mother and son (i.e. \\"I'll take care of you as a child, if you take care of me as an aging parent.\\")  Again, this may not have been intended, but that is what is implied.  I am not the only one who feels this way, since many Amazon reviewers, who gave this book five stars, commented positively on this \\"full circle.\\"  One mother in particular, had a poor relationship with her adult son, and decided to send him this book as a \\"wake up call.\\"  Seriously?  You really need to guilt your son with a children's book into having a relationship with you?  I personally feel that your child did not ask to be born - you chose to have him or her.  I don't like witnessing parenting with expectations.  Love should be given to a child unconditionally, without the expectation thatthe child, when grown, will take care of you in old age.  I took care of my own mother for 15 years when she was ill until her death earlier this year.  I did so gladly, lovingly, and without her expecting me to do so.  I would never want my now 5-month old to feel as if I expect him to take care of me when I'm old.

B004IQMXD4|4.0|As is the case with everything Stevie Nicks does at this point in her career, you know what you're getting right out of the gate so nothing surprises.  That's a sword that cuts both ways and \\"In Your Dreams\\", her latest solo CD, is emblematic of what Nicks has become.  What does that mean?  Well, for starters it means a lot of vague, meandering lines that sound like they were written by a high school sophomore rather than a lyricist.  It also means lots of references to dreams, ghosts, visions, mountains, forests, vampires and what-not that is just so much gobbledygook to listen to.  Let's face it, Nicks' stardom has enabled her to indulge in her precious fantasy world for 40 years and it's gotten repetitive and utterly tiresome.  She claims that her collaborations with producer Dave Stewart consist of poems she wrote that he set to music, which is not exactly how professional song lyricists operate.  Lyrics are meant to be sung.  Cadence, rhythm, syncopation and sound are intrinsic to them in a way not required of poems, which explains why much of this stuff sounds like she's simply reciting lines to a plodding melodic back-beat.  It's amateurish.  So why a 4-star rating?  Well, that sword cuts both ways, remember?  When disciplined, Nicks has a way with both words and melody, as the slight but sturdy \\"Secret Love\\" indicates.  That's one of several gems in this collection that deserves to be called her best in decades.  It's concise, specific and lovely.  \\"For What It's Worth\\" is classic Nicks without all the folderol and co-writer Mike Campbell has set her evocative words to a beautifully gentle melody.  \\"Midnight (A Vampire's Dream)\\" may have a whacked out lyric but it's a solid cut that once again proves Nicks has a knack for pop-craft when she is so inclined.  Best of all is \\"Annabel Lee\\".  Here, Nicks adapts Edgar Allen Poe's rhythmic poem (which is the best lyric found on this CD) and sets it to a sweeping melody totally appropriate to the words.  It ranks with her best work and shows that this would-be hippie sorceress knows what she's doing when she can get out of her own way.    Even some of her songs with Stewart, such as \\"You May Be the One\\", \\"Everybody Loves You\\" and the title tune, manage to click despite some lyrical mishaps.  And \\"My Heart\\", her other Mike Campbell collaboration (unfortunately available only at Barnes and Noble) sounds like Nicks in her heyday.  You can thank Stewart's role as producer for much of that.  He manages to create a soft-rock atmosphere on this CD that at times harkens back to the 70s without pandering to them, and it's clear that Nicks is in her comfort zone with this approach.  Her singing is stronger and more flexible than anyone should expect from a 62-year old woman with her history, and she once again proves that she knows how to scratch and coo the max out of the most convoluted lyrical ideas.  Yes, it's another mixed bag from rock's fairy tale princess, but what else do you expect at this point?  At least this time there is cohesion to the follow through and some very solid cuts to add to the Nicks oeuvre.  Who'd have guessed that at this late date she still had it in her?

B00007K02F|1.0|You don't have to be a Jane Eyre fan to know that the casting of this adaptation is all wrong.   It begins with Anna Paquin, one of the worst actresses in recent memory, as young Jane. While she's not as abominable as she is in later roles, she doesn't start the show off with anything that would make you care about Jane.     While Charlotte Gainsbourge is a tolerable Jane, she's so sappy and spaced-out that it's fairly hard to empathize with her if you don't know the real Jane already.    And then comes William Hurt. Why anyone would cast a man with the emotive range of a cactus to play the dynamic, volatile Mr. Rochester is a mystery. He dribbles out the classic lines like a child who hasn't learned to control its drooling. I don't know how anyone could believe that a brilliant, passionate, and tortured mind could lay behind that deadpan face and dry-as-dust voice. The character should exude energy and brilliance through every move and gesture. I don't know if I can blame Gainsbourge for her flat portrayal of Jane when she had absolutely nothing to play off of.     I'd recommend both the 1983 and 2006 versions to anyone who wants to actually enjoy this story.

B000LPQ6DE|4.0|As a fan of the novel, I felt that the 1983 adaptation starring Timothy Dalton as Rochester was perfect. Zelah Clarke is believable and likable as the sharp-witted Jane, and Dalton plays the brooding, passionate master of Thornfield with passion and panache, doing justice to Rochester's brooding, intense speeches without making them ridiculous.     Being so fond of that very faithful adaptation, my hopes weren't high when I began this one. But since I had memorized the previous one word for word, I tried the 2006 version for a change of pace. I am so glad I did! While it is certainly not as faithful to the original dialogue of Bronte's classic, this version is a wonderful take on the characters of Jane and Rochester. The language is toned down, but the passion is still there!     Toby Stephens plays up the humorous side of Mr. Rochester, which while present in the book has rarely been showcased. He can also brood right along with the best of them. Ruth Wilson plays a believable Jane, but more innocent and less sharp than Zelah Clarke's. She and Toby have fantastic chemistry.    Despite skipping over some of the most moving dialogue in the novel, this adaptation keeps the heart and soul of this story and characters well intact!
B003XREL98|5.0|This novel by E.M. Forster (1879-1970) was first published in 1924. E.M Forster had visited India twice before writing this novel, and had become an opponent of imperialism.     Summary: Adele Quested visits India with Mrs. Moore in order to make up her mind about marrying Mrs. Moore's son Ronny. Adele wants to understand the 'real India', so she befriends an Indian: Dr. Aziz. Mrs. Moore, Adele and Dr. Aziz go on an expedition to the Marabar Caves which turns out to be a disaster. Mrs. Moore undergoes a psychic experience and never recovers from that, and Adele believes she was sexually assaulted by Dr. Aziz. After the arrest of Dr. Aziz tension arrises between the British and the local population in town. On the way back to the UK Adele admits that she was mistaken.     The main themes in this book are the misunderstanding between Adele and Dr. Aziz, the mystery about what really happened during the expedition to the caves and the colonial rule of the British in (then) British India.

B0036S4C6G|4.0|This book could have been alternately titled \\"A bunch of people and what happened to each of them over time\\" (though that would have been much more unwieldy than \\"A Visit From the Goon Squad\\"). Egan does a great job of mapping out the complexities of life as people age. What starts out as a cohesive group of friends or family members gradually disintegrates, disperses and metamorphosizes with time. What happens to people and how those experiences transforms them can almost never be predicted based on their initial states. (Been to a 20th high school reunion lately?)<br /><br />Goon Squad, as other have observed, is less a cohesive novel than a series of short stories or character studies loosely bound together by the relationships between those characters. But these are tasty short stories. Complex and -- most importantly -- real. I never had the sense that I was reading a book; instead, I felt as if I were eavesdropping on overhead conversations. If you can pull that off, you've made it as an author.<br /><br />Lives rarely go as planned. There are detours and disappointments aplenty. The promising fall into irretrievable depths, while the mediocre sometimes find their way to unexpected success and satisfaction. How do these things happen? Why? Good literature like \\"Goon Squad\\" helps us to ponder these eternal questions by giving us an intimate window into the lives of our fellow travelers.

B002BSHXJA|5.0|First, if you are in the market for an INCREDIBLE box set, you found one. As far as I know, this is the ONLY TIME ALL THE OFFICIAL VERSIONS OF THE LP ARTWORK HAVE BEEN REPRODUCED IN OVER 40 YEARS. (except for ABBEY RD, YELLOW SUBMARINE and LET IT BE, of course.) Even in the era of LPS, reprints of SGT PEPPER on vinyl removed the light pink-to red cloud design created by THE FOOL, for the inner paper sleeve. Later, the Sgt Pepper cutout insert was no longer in new Vinyl editions. In many reissues, MAGICAL MYSTERY TOUR LPs took the booklet out later LP reprints. Even some reprints of the LP artwork for the WHITE ALBUM changed, like the embossed title (in later vinyl LPS, \\"THE BEATLES\\" is just printed in grey ink. Even the upward facing cut to remove the WHITE ALBUM LPs (well, CDS in this case) has been restored on the MONO BOX. When the artwork for those newly remastered CDs of 2009 was designed, the CD package layout changed drastically in every case, when compaired to the original LP artwork. Sure, the front covers look the same as the LPs, but that's it. SO, if you want to own the ORIGINAL LP ARTWORK in CD sized duplication, this is it. (Except for 3 album titles previously listed, which were only stereo mixes.) These lps dont even list the songs as \\"Tracks\\". Everything is just like the first printings of the LPs, believe it.  This BOX is as close to the original LP artwork as you are going to get. So, that is the first thing to consider, when you decide if you want to buy a cheap reproduction, or the offical EMI product. The fake boxsets changed the artwork, to make a cheaper product. The fake boxsets ended up removing many of the extras touches that make these reproductions so authenic, and worth collecting.<br />. Let us consider whats different between the fakes and the authenic boxsets.<br /><br />1. The Paper inner sleeves are missing, especially the SGT PEPPER pink/red inner sleeve. All the real mono boxset CDS have these.<br />2. The MAGICIAL MYSTERY TOUR booklet is gone, and the fake CD cover doesnt have the LP<br /> subscript beneath the photo of the Beatles in animal costumes, \\"includes 24-page full color picture book.\\"<br />3. Poor manufacturing of the fake CD covers overall. FAKE WHITE ALBUM LP replicas opens on the side, not the top of the cover.<br />4. The CD titles usually arent in chronological order, when you open a new boxset. EMI's boxsets have CDS arranged by year, PLEASE PLEASE ME to MONO MASTERS.<br />5. The plastic inner CD sleeves of the fakes are very flimsy, and tear easily. The real ones are made better, & fit snug.<br />6. Blurry reproduction of FAKE boxset artwork, possibly using cheap ink. (bad smell reported upon first opening CDs.)<br />7. Some mono CDs are reportedly not playing properly in some models of CD-player.<br /><br />As for the mono mixes, some of the early albums sound MUCH BETTER in mono. The DOOM OF EARLY STEREO SEPERATION cursed the early Beatle's albums. You would hear drums, bass, rhythym guitar, and other sonic bits in LEFT CHANNEL, while the right channel would have the vocals, maybe a little percussion, and the lead instrument. You cant listen to that in earphones, cos the modern ear has been trained to hear the Bass and Bass drum in the CENTER of the stereo picture, for over 40 years. MONO restores that natural posistion of the bass elements. And, by playing the mono CDs' bass thru TWO woofers, instead of one, the bass punch is doubled. This is why MONO mixes in general, are said to be more \\"punchy\\", and have more presence. (cos on stage, bass drum and bass notes are in the center as well.) There is another PRACTICAL REASON why mono sound is very handy. AT BEATLES PARTIES, PLAY THE MONO VERSIONS, SO EVERYBODY IN THE ROOM HEARS THE SAME SONG. With stereo albums that have a lot of \\"movement\\", or swirling effects between the speakers, or might still have poor balance on the bottom end, depending on your relationship to the two (or five) speaker placements, the song you willhear wont have all the elements. One person near the left speaker might hear a drum and bass mix of DAY TRIPPER, and another person might hear an accapello version of THIS BOY. Therefore at parties, es, MONO IS COOL. (This only occured to me tonight, when i was walking around my room, away from the \\"perfect posistion\\" chair for the stereo sonic picture. EVERYPLACE in my room had the SAME SONIC PICTURE. Cool.)<br /><br />Naturally, most of us want to get our hands on MONO versions of WHITE ALBUMS, SGT PEPPER, MAGICAL MYSTERY TOUR, and  REVOLVER. EACH SONG is a different mix, compared to the stereo mixes. Since on the psychedelic work, one channel always seemed to have been devoted to either sound effects, which by then moved between the speakers, in order to reproduce the effects of aural hallucinations upon the listener. And, that's cool and valid, dont get me wrong there. But, each mono mix was accomplished completely seperately. When it comes to the basic levels of the major instruments, they remain the same from mono to stereo. Nevertheless changes snuck in. In mono, \\"SHES LEAVING HOME\\" plays faster by a semitone. I'm ONLY SLEEPING has different backround guitar leads. SGT PEPPER (reprise) is very different, with lots of stage banter by Paul. I remember hearing cowbells appear in new places, one song is shorter by a minute (HELTER SKELTER), and some songs are longer (Got to get you into my life, Dont Make Me Cry). Some songs sound Very Heavy, very INTENSE in mono, like YER BLUES. POW! It's really punchy and dirty in mono, and REVOLUTION 2 (rock version) sounds more dense, like HARD ROCK, like JOHN wanted. (HE didnt like the stereo mix of REVOLUTION 2.) THE WHITE ALBUM differs all over the place...a little tambourine here, a bit of echo there. Usually, the sound effects (PIGGIES, BLACKBIRD, BACK IN THE USSR, etc) get changed, since those knobs were turned in such a way, that each mix was an individual performance. On the MONO PAST MASTERS CDs in this boxset the original songs from the YELLOW SUBMARINE LP appear in mono, to replace the stereo only songs from the STEREO version of the title. ITS ONLY A NORTHERN SONG has a VERY different mono mix, as does ITS ALL TOO MUCH, compared to the stereo.<br /><br />Lets face it...MONO BOXSET is the OBVIOUS collector's dream, unless this set comes out on vinyl. (hahaHAHAhaha-HAH!) Recently, it appears that the Original MONO BOX SETS are being liquidated, so as to be removed from the market. Limiting availability increases its \\"collectability\\". I doubt EMI-APPLE will release these mono CDs as individual titles; if it DOES happen it wont be in this format. (ie Mini LP reproductions, MONO Mix). The problem with the market getting flooded with cheaply made, inferior quality MONO BOXSETS shouldnt worry the discerning shopper. Right now, these last MONO box sets are getting liquidated. I spent only about \\"one Benjamin\\" for a NEW MONO BOX from one of Amazon's largest drop sellers. When these MONO boxsets are gone, EMI might not reprint--who knows? If you are EVER going to get one of these, this might be the only chance you have, before you have to buy NEW copies from collectors, with the price jacked up way beyond the original price. Since HELP and RUBBER SOUL appear here in both mono and stereo versions, at least you wont have to buy the 2009 stereo remasters of those 2 albums, to replace your old 1987 mastered copies. Plus, these are the ORIGINAL LP STEREO mixes of HELP and RUBBER SOUL, not the new mixes George Martin made for the first CD release of those two titles. Do you still need more persuasion to splurge? It was a smart way to advertise Reese's Peanut Butter Cups, which considered itself an ideal marriage of ingredients that most people would never have dreamed of.<br /><br />PRESENTATIONZEN is a lot like that commercial.  You read it and feel as though four people, each intently oblivious in his own world, have crashed at a 4-way juncture, blending How-To with Self-Help, Philosophy, and Art.  Let's start with Art.  You can save five bucks and buy the Kindle version of this book if you wish, but I don't know why you would, so beautiful is the print version with its glossy photos of zen gardens and lovely slides showing many arresting images by way of example for those wishing to make Power Point presentations.<br /><br />The philosophical aspect comes from the \\"Zen\\" you see in the title.  Author Garr Reynolds, an expat resident of Japan himself, constantly weaves Zen-like wisdom into his advice by discussing the beauty of simplicity, the need for a less-is-more approach, and other bits of wisdom designed to thwart \\"death by Power Point\\" (we've all been there).  This ties in with the \\"How-To.\\"  In simple steps, the book shows how you can make a live presentation, whether you work in business, education, or the public sector.  Finally there's a dose of self-help to the book.  Reynolds counsels would-be presenters on the need to get in touch with the child within all of us, the need to take risks, the necessity of connecting with the audience by ignoring negative voices that whisper fears of failure.<br /><br />Each chapter ends with a section called \\"In Sum\\" which offers the highlights of what you read.  Many visuals are provided as well.  The only annoyance is that, sometimes, they are crowded together on a page with copy that you can barely read.<br /><br />Overall, a wonderfully simple book with complex considerations.  It should be required reading for all businessmen especially.  By the nature of their job and its expectations, they are the worst offenders when it comes to carnage through Power Point (Professor Plum did it in the board room with a slide show).  If their boss's won't buy them a copy, maybe their beleaguered audiences will.  It's all in the name of mercy and Zen.usinessmen especially. 1400064287|5.0|Made to Stick by Chip Heath and Dan Heath (Random House, 2008) Reviewed by Steve Gladis, Ph.D., July 2011<br />Overview: Making ideas last or, as the authors put it, \\"stick,\\" is both simple and compelling. It's simple if you know six basic principles and compelling if you're on the other side of someone using these powerful strategies. The authors (brothers and faculty members--one at Duke, the other at Stanford) are no novices to best sellers; just check out their other blockbuster, Switch.  The focus of Made to Stick is clearly on influence. While not new, the concept and their explanations of the principles will be new to many readers. They cover six major principles of influence or \\"stickiness\\": 1) Simplicity: The Kiss principle...keep it simple, stupid; 2) Unexpectedness: Shock, surprise, and attention-getting tactics stick in the mind of the audience (however large or small) and are the staple of every good public speaker; 3) Concreteness: Making it real in detail makes it stick; 4) Credibility: Reputation is the key, and getting people to believe in you makes your words stick; 5) Emotional: All decisions ultimately rest on how we feel about something. Deal with emotions or they'll deal with you; 6) Stories: At our core, we're tribal folk who rely on stories to understand the world. And when it comes to sticking, the Heaths have created a book that will stick around for many years. An easy mnemonic to remember this process is SUCCESs: Simple-Unexpected-Concrete-Credentialed-Emotional-Story-and \\"s\\" just to make the word spell right!

Coraline faces life as it comes, the good, bad, and indifferent, with a courageous honesty that belongs almost uniquely to childhood. She is herself, whether she is bored and annoyed with her everyday parents, or staking all their lives on her ability to outwit the unimaginably evil Other Mother creature.<br /><br />Children have this balance of imagination and pragmatism, and incorporate both in their creative play. This story captures that attitude perfectly, so you're swept into Coraline's perspective on her little world which so unexpectedly becomes larger and darker one rainy day.<br /><br />There are profound philosophical points that could be construed as morals of the tale, but the story stands on its own; it is great for its own sake, not just for the lessons it holds.<br /><br />I very much look forward to sharing this excellent story with my son.

B000063DFQ|4.0|By the end of the Beast on the Road tour, it was clear that Clive Burr didn't have the stamina to be able to withstand the tough schedule that Maiden had been accustomed to since his recruitment for the debut album 3 years previously. Once again, the band had a change in lineup and settled on former Trust drummer Nicko McBrain, whom they had known as a result of touring together within the past year or so.<br /><br /> From the opening salvo of Where Eagles Dare, its apparent that Nicko was just as strong as Clive, if not even more energetic and dexterous in his abilities as a player, to become a worthy replacement and, with the passing of time, a key element to the band's overall sound. Bruce Dickinson comes into his own on the second release with Maiden and instantly its apparent that he surpasses Paul Di'Anno by leaps and bounds not only vocally but as a songwriter specifically; and Revelations follows in with an arrangement that builds and grows beautifully and displays the best elements of the band with Steve Harris' pulsating bass and the twin guitar attack of Dave Murray and Adrian Smith coupled with Dickinson's lyrical concepts that contain an otherworldly sense of imagery.<br /><br />Track 3 would become a Maiden staple in the Flight of Icarus, penned by Adrian and Bruce, this number would be one of many songs that would establish them as a powerful songwriting team within Maiden and sustain it for the next several years, if not into the present day. Die with Your Boots On consolidates the album thus far with a powerful precision in tempo and the by-now classic elements of what defines the sound of Maiden. The Trooper is a classic number penned by Steve Harris and is yet another quintessential tune that has lasted throughout the years as prime Maiden. Still Life is one of those rare Murray/Harris collaborations that adorn a Maiden release every so often, and although Dave may not be as prolific of a songwriter, when he does present a song, its easily up to par with the regular inputs from Dickinson, Smith and Harris. Quest for Fire although, is a bit of a mid-paced number and has a slight touch of filler here, but within the collection of material presented thus far, this is easily forgiven. Sun and Steel comes next and is another melodic rocker from Adrian and Bruce and to me, personally, is another number that slips through the wealth of the Maiden catalogue as a hidden gem. Harris completes the album cycle with To Tame a Land and continues the trend of ending each record with a longer track ala Phantom of the Opera, Hallowed By Thy Name, etc. Although an interesting track, its a bit lacking in the hook dept. and seems a bit labored in spots.<br /><br />Overall, Piece of Mind is a consistent and even better follow-up to 82's Number of the Beast and would begin a string of quality albums that would sustain this particular lineup for the next 5 years, and even though today they have managed to turn out quality records since the reunion, with the addition of Janick Gers, the 83-89 lineup is best known and remembered as the most fruiful period and classic lineup for this band. 4 and half stars.ular inputs from Dickinson, Smith and Harris. B00000AFDW|3.0|When you hear the opening bars and vocals of `Psycho Circus' burst out of the speakers for the first time, it is a wondrous moment. It sounds like Kiss, having reformed the original line-up at the end of the 90s after a decade and a half apart, have actually pulled it off. For five minutes and ten seconds of glorious stomping glam rock and roll, it feels like they have returned to their heyday form. But alas, after this superb classic Kiss opener, 1998's `Psycho Circus' somewhat implodes.<br /><br />It would have seemed sensible at this point in their story to employ a producer from their classic heavy period, such as Bob Ezrin (`[[ASIN:B000001EL3 Destroyer]]', `[[ASIN:B000001FZP Revenge]]') or Larry Kramer (`[[ASIN:B000001EL5 Rock & Roll Over]]'). Instead they went with Bruce Fairbairn who had mostly helmed albums for glossy mid rockers like Bon Jovi, Aerosmith, Poison and Van Halen, as well as The Cranberries. Consequently, with the Kiss sound on this album augmented by keyboards and effects that would never have appeared on one of their records the first time around, the band sounds watered down and over-produced. Then there is the additional problem that too often the songs bear little resemblance to the music they would have made in the 70s.<br /><br />That said, none of the songs are terrible. It is also the third Kiss album in a row that showcases strong, well-crafted, largely non-corny songs from Gene Simmons in a late career renaissance. In fact, his are mostly the best of the album - the moody `Within' and `Journey of 1,000 Years' are truly fine, if unrepresentative of the Kiss sound. And the folksy `We Are One' sounds like it could have been a good track on his 1978 solo album. Though they again fall into the tired trap of trying to re-write `Rock and Roll All Nite' (twice), his version - `You Wanted the Best' is ok (if hokey) for what it is. Paul Stanley's `I Pledge Allegiance to the State of Rock & Roll' is far superior. While Stanley's title track is far and away the best song on the album, and `Dreamin' is excellent as well, he is also behind a few clunkers. `Raise Your Glasses', written with corporate writer Holly Knight, doesn't sit quite right with the set and the Peter Criss voiced `I Finally Found My Way', with its tinkling piano and country crooner leanings, is woefully wrong and out of place. On the other hand, Ace Frehley's only contribution, `Into the Void', is convincing.<br /><br />Sadly, though much more satisfying than any of their albums without make-up, the bottom line is that `Psycho Circus' was just too unrepresentative and muddled in approach - and too calculated - to resuscitate the glory days. It is true that Kiss had almost run out of puff on more than one occasion (1985 with `[[ASIN:B00000AF96 Asylum]]', 1989 with `[[ASIN:B000001FQQ Hot in the Shade]]'), but `Carnival of Souls' is a different kettle of fish. It could not be further from the Kiss of the 70s or indeed the Kiss of the 80s. It sounds muddy, it is heavy and dirgey. The subject matter is almost nihilistic at times (`Hate', `Jungle' - both excellent) and the vocals have a 90s drawl to them. If 1992's heavy, dark and dirty `[[ASIN:B000001FZP Revenge]]' lacked a sense of fun, `Carnival of Souls' was close to miserable in comparison.<br /><br />Recorded somewhere around 1995, the frivolousness with which Kiss were equated was most definitely `out'. It was a time when the sounds of grunge, indie rock and grunge metal were the approach that gave a band cred. `Carnival of Souls' was coproduced by Toby Wright who was aligned with the likes of Alice in Chains, Slayer and Queensryche. So Kiss' intentions on this effort are pretty easy to read. Measured against those benchmarks, `Carnival of Souls' could easily be dismissed as a wanna-be effort. But measured against other Kiss albums, it is a far more intriguing creature.<br /><br />This album is the band's greatest departure and contains some of their most interesting and unusual songwriting. They also go back to Kiss basics by backing one another on vocals and relinquishing the last dozen years of screeching in favour of their lower registers. Bruce Kulick even sings the final track (`I Walk Alone'). A great (and unusual) thing about it is that the six tracks co-written by Gene Simmons are all well crafted and among the strongest of his career. In abandoning silly sex romps as a subject matter, he seems to have really risen to the occasion and equaled himself with the ever-reliable Stanley. `Hate', `Childhood's End', `In My Head' and `Seduction of the Innocent' are all dark, durgey grunge metal with occasional melodic flourishes thrown in. Stanley's six songs are also good. The funked-up grunge of `Jungle' is excellent and, if it weren't for the subject matter, the closest to sounding something like a tradition Kiss song. The morose ballad, `I Will Be There' is also outstanding - a vast improvement on the equivalent on 1992's `Revenge'.<br /><br />`Carnival of Souls' is a dislocating experience for the most part. It takes a lot of getting used to and is initially hard to get all the way through in one listen. It sounds under-mixed and it definitely doesn't sound like Kiss - which is probably why it has been so badly received - no pop rock melodies or fist-raising anthems here. Dark and gloomy, it is the only real heavy metal album they ever recorded. But if you are prepared to stick with it, you realize it is not only the riskiest and most intelligent thing they ever made, but quite riveting on its own unique terms.nal melodic flourishes thrown in. Dark and gloomy, it is the only real heavy metal album they ever recorded. But if you are prepared to stick with it, you realize it is not only the riskiest and most intelligent thing they ever made, but quite riveting on its own unique terms.

B003FVI2KQ|5.0|The 1090T (or the slightly faster 1100T) will provide most of the speed you'd get out of a $900 top-of-the-line Intel i7, at about 20% of the cost. Add to that the features standard on AMD 880-based (and also cheaper than Intel) motherboards, and you've got an excellent foundation for a gaming rig on a budget -- or a somewhat future-proof general purpose rig.    The ONLY issue you may encounter is that of heat if you go to overclock it (and I'm guessing you want to, if you're buying a Black Edition CPU). I am able to get 3.6 GHz (+400 MHz) just by disabling TurboCore and tweaking the multiplier in BIOS. 3.7 requires a minimal bump in voltage. 4.0 is perfectly stable at 1.44V, but the stock cooler is not up to the task. Stress testing pushes the Core temps into the 62C range in a matter of minutes, resulting in a blue screen. I do not see this as a failure of the CPU itself, and the stock cooling is adequate for the official speed ratings and somewhat beyond. Brief tests revealed that my particular CPU may be good as high as 4.23 GHz, but until I can attach better cooling, I will not even think about it. Still, even 4.0 is 25% over the rated speed, and will get you back most of the raw number crunching you are giving up by not getting an i7 Extreme.    Drop $40 or so on a Cooler Master Hyper 212 and a second fan, and you most likely will cruise at 4 GHz without raising a tremendous racket or having to invest in watercooling. Sure it's not the bleeding edge of performance, but it's reasonably close and at a price most of us can live with. Since the TurboCore feature kicks up the voltage (to 1.475V) and frequency (to 3.6 GHz) when 3 or fewer cores are loaded, it seems pretty apparent the only obstacle to continuous 3.6 GHz operation of all six cores is that the stock heatsink is JUST adequate for it. The CPU doesn't need anywhere NEAR 1.475V to run at 3.6 GHz, and you may find that turning OFF TurboCore and bumping the multiplier to 18.0 for all six cores actually results in cooler and faster operation. I did not have to bump up the voltage AT ALL from the stock 1.375V to run at 3.6GHz.    Even if you decide not to overclock it (which would be a shame, being a Black Edition), it's still screaming fast at 3.2 GHz. I built this rig to do two things: (1) be an audio workstation, and (2) play Minecraft at 2048x1152, and it does both of these things quite respectably. It's a HUGE upgrade from my dual-core 2.4 GHz Athlon 64, which was getting long in the tooth (but still works perfectly).    Get a decent motherboard to underpin it (you can afford to, the CPU is reasonably priced) and you can take this CPU a long, long way. The value is tremendous. I can't really recommend the 1090T over the 1100T, being that the price difference is a mere $10, but I don't think you'd be disappointed in either one. If you want to squeeze still more speed out of it, go with 1600 MHz or 2000 MHz DDR3 memory.

0553386794|4.0|Often, with epic fantasy, it is \\"do or die\\" with regard to reviews. You get masses of people that love a particular author, or you get masses that hate them. Then, it becomes a mini-ratings war as to which reviews dominate (See: Terry Goodkind/Robert Jordan/ George R.R. Martin).<br /><br />I expected Game of Thrones to be an epic fantasy series without par, given all of the hype surrounding the series particularly in light of its HBO feature. What I found, instead, was a series that holds a large amount of promise, yet also has a few drawbacks.<br /><br />PROS:<br /><br />-George R.R. Martin weaves a tale using a myriad of characters. There is no predominant character or storyline. (This is viewed as a positive given the sheer scope of the work).<br /><br />-No character is fully good or bad. Variegated shades of grey characterize the personalities of all but a few characters.<br /><br />-Martin is (shock!) willing to kill off characters that the reader has fallen to love. Many epic fantasy series (read: almost ALL) go along without deaths among the cast of main characters. The key with this work, however, is that you are attached to so many of the characters that it becomes okay that one of your favorites has perished. This also helps with an emotional attachment to the storyline.<br /><br />-Martin is, without a doubt, a masterful writer. The prose is beautiful.<br /><br />-If you like tales of knights, with chivalry, and little magical interference, this is the book for you.<br /><br />-The political intrigue in this book is intense, leaving one guessing the entire scope of the book, peeling layer upon layer of mystery down until finally the truth is revealed. Or is it?<br /><br />-This volume will leave you wanting more with the second book in the series, despite the fact that the book is a lengthy read. However, as mentioned, his prose reads easily.<br /><br />-Realism. I view this as both a pro and a con. You become attached to the characters. The world is not \\"chivalrous\\" despite the appearance some characters give. Swearing, blood, gore, sex, etc. are highly present and brutally detailed. The book is written very realistically in a world that appears similar to our own at an earlier era (with obvious exceptions). Thus, this does not feature new creative worlds, races, magic systems, etc, but a brutal realism.<br /><br />CONS:<br /><br />-For me at least, this book was not as creative as some of the other epic fantasy authors I have read (Sanderson in particular, Jordan). Don't get me wrong. The intrigue in this book alone makes it worth the read; it is a brilliant work, yet I find myself drawn to unusual worlds, unusual systems. However, if you are not looking for such creative, unusual worlds, then this remains the book for you. Again, this is a minor \\"con,\\" as I enjoyed the book greatly.<br /><br />-As mentioned, if you enjoy a main character, or if you enjoy a \\"clean\\" epic fantasy, do not pick up this title. It isn't for you. However, if you have doubts, I urge you to give it a try.<br /><br />All in all, this was a very promising start to an epic fantasy series. It is a realistic title, engrossing, and masterfully written. I will reserve judgment on its relation to other epic fantasy authors (Goodkind, Jordan, Sanderson, Brooks) because, well, we all enjoy different things in our fantasy. Thus, it is not for me to say that Martin is better or worse than any of the aforementioned authors, simply that they all share the same league, in my eyes, albeit for different subgenres within their genre., sex, etc. are highly present and brutally detailed. The intrigue in this book alone makes it worth the read; it is a brilliant work, yet I find myself drawn to unusual worlds, unusual systems. B002ZG99OK|3.0|It's hard to rate a movie like Zookeeper.  On the one hand, no one's going to say that it's a must-see comedy. But on the other hand, it's better than the average film of this sort, and while it's completely predictable, it does manage to keep you entertained and you still laugh even when you know what's coming. And some of the scenes are true comic gold, even if the film as a whole can't maintain that high level.<br /><br />The plot:  Griffin Keyes (Kevin James) is a zookeeper at the Franklin Park Zoo in Boston, a job which he loves but which gets him little respect. Particularly from his girlfriend Stephanie (Leslie Bibb), who dumps him at the beginning of the movie just as he's in the middle of his elaborately planned proposal of marriage, stating that she just can't see herself getting serious with a zookeeper. Crushed, Griffin briefly considers quitting his job and going to work for his brother, Dave (Nat Faxon), who has a high-end car dealership, but Kate (Rosario Dawson), the zoo's vet, talks him out of it. Five years later, when Dave is getting married, Stephanie suddenly reappears. Griffin is just as smitten with her as he was five years before. Now he's seriously considering quitting in order to win her, enough so that the zoo animals - who consider him the best keeper they've ever had - decide to take action. Starting with revealing to a very startled Griffin that they can in fact talk. Knowing that the only way to get Griffin to stay is to help him win Stephanie, they proceed to bombard him with all kinds of mating advice, varying according to the animal giving it. Griffin's attempts to follow their advice have, as one might expect, highly variable and sometimes disastrous results.<br /><br />Admittedly, high comedy or classic comedy, Zookeeper is not. It's one-hundred percent predictable but you still laugh, mainly because of the talented cast (live and voice actors), who manage to keep things lively enough that you still want to see things unfold in spite of knowing what will happen. Kevin James is perfect as the hapless Griffin (you know you're hapless when you're taking dating advice from talking animals). Leslie Bibb does a nice turn as the gorgeous but devastatingly shallow Stephanie.  And Joe Rogan is deliciously obnoxious as Stephanie's uber-testosteroned boyfriend Gale.<br /><br />But hey, Zookeeper is really about the talking animals and the film's technical and animal-handling crew did an excellent job, both in making it look like they're actually talking and in managing to give them a lot of physical expressiveness in their roles. The voice-actors behind the live animals were superbly cast and all did a great job of bringing their animal characters to life:  Nick Nolte's gorilla Bernie gives the film a lot of its best moments; Adam Sandler's cheeky monkey Donald, who's always lording his opposable thumbs over the other animals and whose fall-back advice for any situation is \\"throw poo at them\\"; the mated lions - Sylvester Stallone's Joe and Cher's Janet - have some of the best dialogue in the film, arguing like a real married couple over who's better suited to give advice; Judd Apatow's elephant Barry has a fear of confrontation and a hay-eating disorder; Jon Favreau (yeah, that Jon Favreau, director of Iron Man I & II) and Faizon Love are Jerome and Bruce, a couple of male bears constantly ranking on each other, are a riot; and Don Rickles gets in some particularly bad advice as a frog.<br /><br />There are a few let downs, largely I feel to weaknesses in the script. While some scenes are brilliantly funny, others don't quite hit the mark and a few simply go on too long. And while most of the characters are decently written, a few  were either poorly thought out or inadequately developed. Ken Jeong, who was brilliantly funny in films like Knocked Up, Role Models and The Hangover movies, never quite hits the mark as Venom, a co-worker of Griffin's whose social skills make everyone around him cringe. Donnie Wahlberg is wasted as Shane, a cardboard bad-guy who mistreats one of the animals at the zoo.  And the extremely talented Rosario Dawson, whose veteranarian Kate is key to the plot, isn't really fleshed out enough to make her relationship with Griffin really believeable.<br /><br />But all that said, Zookeeper remains a decent film that meets the expectations the trailers set up.  Recommended as a light comedy with a few memorable scenes, a pleasant enough way to pass a couple of hours., a cardboard bad-guy who mistreats one of the animals at the zoo. 0553582038|5.0|[This review contains no major spoilers for A Feast for Crows, but it does outline the basic premise of a few characters' storylines and mention the identity of new point-of-view characters.  There are major spoilers for earlier books in the series as well.]<br /><br />The contrast between A Feast for Crows and its immediate predecessor, A Storm of Swords, could scarcely be greater.  Narrowly the shortest book in the series (to date) as Storm is narrowly the longest, Feast is about the peace following the war that was so violently and definitively resolved in the earlier book.  And, because of unforeseen complications in the structure of the series, Feast only features about half the characters from earlier books.  This changed to a limited scope and a much less rapid pace has caused many fans to treat the book as a failure, easily the weakest in the series.  As I've said, I go against the grain a little by regarding Storm as the weakest.  I can now add that I go against the grain that much further, in thinking that A Feast for Crows is, given its inherent limitations, the best installment of A Song of Ice and Fire so far.<br /><br />One of the problems long-time fans have is the introduction of many new point of view characters, and the relative absence of familiar ones.  It's true that 27 of the book's 45 chapters feature new points of view, and an additional 12 feature characters only introduced in the most recent book.  Inevitably there's some adjustment to be made to the lack of well-known voices, but I find the new points of view so fascinating that it's well worth the effort.<br /><br />The most prominent character in the book, old or new, is Cersei, who is, after the deaths of her son Joffrey and her father Tywin, the unquestioned Queen Regent of the Seven Kingdoms.  The war seems to be over and her other son Tommen's throne secure, but Cersei is far from relieved.  She believes the Tyrells, whose support helped her family end the war, are now scheming against her, and the (incorrect) notion that her dwarf brother Tyrion murdered Joffrey has reawakened older, stranger fears.  Cersei has always been unstable, but her insanity reaches new heights in A Feast for Crows, and her reflections on previous events in the series expand the character and make her more dramatically credible without obscuring her cruelty.  This unreliable narration substantially heightens the novel's mood, of which more later.<br /><br />The other major new point of view character is Brienne of Tarth, whose quest to find Sansa Stark is essentially all she has to live for.  As readers we know where Sansa is, and therefore can recognize just how hopeless Brienne's search is.  This could make her chapters frustrating, but the milieu in which they occur is compelling enough that the seeming irrelevance of the plot becomes a minor annoyance.  Like Jaime and Arya's chapters in A Storm of Swords, Brienne's storyline shows us the effect of the war on the riverlands, and like those chapters it's plagued with unexpected, highly coincidental reappearance of familiar secondary characters.  But there are striking new characters as well, and the riverlands are now rebuilding in the aftermath of war, so the tragedy is tinged with hope.<br /><br />In addition to Cersei and Brienne, there are nine chapters featuring six new minor point of view characters, who provide a window into events on the Iron Islands and in Dorne.  These chapters have also been controversial, with some complaining that their relevance to the overall story is not, as of this book, particularly clear.  That's true enough, but in a transitional novel I can forgive the presence of a few dangling threads, especially when the characters and locations are as rich as in these chapters.  The scorched land of Dorne, its gout-ridden, cautious Prince Doran, and the daughters and nieces who urge him to throw himself into the game of thrones, are particular favorites, and the vicious lifestyle and Lovecraftian religion of the Iron Islands are, as ever, darkly compelling.<br /><br />Imagery of metaphorical crows feeding on carrion is prominent in the book, perhaps excessively so, and the Seven Kingdoms are indeed so exhausted by the recent war that only crows can be truly satisfied.  And amongst the tentative efforts at rebuilding are forewarnings of the horror to come, creating an atmosphere of prophecy, magic, mental decay, and encroaching doom.  A Feast for Crows may not have the scope and speed of A Storm of Swords, but it more than makes up for that loss with a vividly-evoked portrait of characters and a society perched on the edge of a knife, thinking safety is in reach, but actually about to be skewered.s ever, darkly compelling. There are major spoilers for A Game of Thrones and A Clash of Kings as well.]<br /><br />More than 400,000 words long and over 1,000 pages in hardcover, A Storm of Swords is, albeit narrowly, the longest book to date in George R. R. Martin's A Song of Ice and Fire.  It's also regarded by many or most of the series' fans as the best book yet, full of the gritty detail, unexpected twists, and shocking deaths that set the series apart from other contemporary epic fantasy.  Full the book certainly is, and its most momentous scenes are every bit as wonderful as the fan reaction suggests.  But I must confess that to my mind A Storm of Swords is, not the best book in the series so far, but the worst.<br /><br />Part of the problem is simply its length.  There's often a desire among readers to see each book in a series become thicker than the last, and most of the time I share that desire.  But 400,000 words' worth of the level of detail Martin provides is something else altogether.  It's not that any particular storyline, historical detail, character, location, or miscellaneous piece of description is dull or extraneous; it's that, taken together, they cause the book to bog down a little, in spite of the rapid resolution of so many storylines.<br /><br />Another distracting element is a reliance on unlikely narrative coincidence.  Despite the vast size of Westeros and the scope of the story, point-of-view characters cross paths with each other and with minor supporting characters surprisingly often.  The issue here is that Martin has introduced some fascinating secondary protagonists and needs to keep them in the story even when they move away from the primary characters who previously provided our perspective on them.  So, out all of the people in all the kingdoms they could encounter, they walk into some of the other primary characters.  It's not a fatal flaw, and in a story of this size some contrivance is forgivable, but it is distracting, and runs counter to the realism for which the series is justly praised.<br /><br />Much of the coincidence and path-crossing involves events in the riverlands, where the bulk of the fighting in this War of the Five Kings has taken place.  Unlike some fantasy and historical novels, which treat war and conquest as a game for aristocrats and ignore the consequences for ordinary people, A Song of Ice and Fire is at pains to show the disease, destruction, and death that peasants suffer when the powerful cross swords.  This is a noble intention, but I can't help feeling that A Storm of Swords takes it too far, to a point where readers may be frustrated rather than inspired to thought or sympathy.  One character spends most of the novel bouncing helplessly between one miserable situation and another, his or her storyline advancing very little.<br /><br />There's another character who also provides a perspective on the riverlands, but his storyline is anything but static.  Jaime Lannister becomes a point of view character in A Storm of Swords, and in terms of psychological complexity he's perhaps Martin's richest creation to date.  In the aftermath of a long imprisonment the power and arrogance that have prevented Jaime from confronting the egregiousness of his own behavior are diminished, and his grudging respect for Brienne of Tarth, his captor as he crosses the riverlands, leads him to reexamine some of his infamous crimes.  It's nothing so cheap and straightforward as a redemptive arc-- he remains Jaime Lannister, proud, snide, and capable of viciousness-- but it does enrich his characterization, adding notes of tragedy and contemplation to what had previously been a monstrous villain.<br /><br />Regardless of which side they're on in the greatconflict, for many of the series' established characters the events of A Storm of Swords are dark ones.  Jaime's brother Tyrion remains a point of view character, and in addition to the disfigurement he suffered at the end of the previous book, he has to deal with his father's return to the city, displacing Tyrion from his powerful position as the Hand of the King.  Uglier than ever, and unappreciated for his contributions to the successful defense of the city, Tyrion finds his situation going from bad to worse as the novel progresses.<br /><br />Mother to one of the other contenders for the throne, Catelyn Stark has her own troubles as the novel opens: a dying father, worry over her son's fate in battle, and guilt over her secret release of Jaime Lannister in the hope of recovering her daughters.  When Robb returns, he brings news that makes the desperate situation of House Stark even worse.  The youthful King in the North has won every battle, and yet finds himself in danger of losing the war.<br /><br />Separated by distance from the intrigues of southern Westeros, Jon Snow and Daenerys Targaryen continue their isolated storylines.  Jon is posing as one of the wildlings to bring information about their intentions back to his brothers on the Night's Watch, but he discovers that keeping to his vows while posing as a spy may not be as easy as he had believed.  The unusual ethics and (sometimes literally) larger-than-life behavior of the wildlings enriches the astonishingly deep milieu of the series yet further.  As does Slaver's Bay, the region in which Daenerys arrives early in the book.  In need of an army, she considers buying the fiercely loyal, viciously trained troops known as the Unsullied from the slave city Astapor.  But such a purchase demands a high price, and the young queen's way of paying it may bring her more trouble than triumph.<br /><br />All these storylines, and several others, evolve toward a major turning point, the end of the first movement in George R. R. Martin's very long story and the beginning of the second.  The last third of the novel is so loaded with vivid sequences and jaw-dropping developments that my other complaints come to seem like quibbling.  I do believe that A Storm of Swords is the least of the first five books in A Song of Ice and Fire, but that simply means it's the weakest length in a remarkably strong chain.haracters the events of A Storm of Swords are dark ones. 0553136321|5.0|John LeCarre's 1974 novel \\"Tinker, Tailer, Soldier, Spy\\" is a stunningly good dramatization of a real incident of treason inside the British Secret Intelligence Service, and another in the author's series of excellent Cold War novels.<br /><br />As the novel begins, George Smiley, veteran British intelligence agent, is recalled from unhappy retirement to deal with new information concerning a possible mole inside the Secret Intelligence Service.  Smiley is commissioned by a senior civil servant to run his own investigation, off the grid, to identify and trap the traitor.<br /><br />The trail of clues will lead Smiley back through the circumstances that led to his own forced retirement, a botched mission in Czechoslovakia in which an agent was shot and the head of the Intelligence Service discredited.  It becomes clear that the mission was betrayed by an insider.<br /><br />The balance of the novel is Smiley's meticulous hunt for that insider, who is a traitor sent by Moscow to infiltrate the Service. LeCarre's prose allows the reader to take in the clues over Smiley's shoulder; the tension at the end is practically unbearable as Smiley springs a final trap that will reveal the surprising identity of the traitor.<br /><br />\\"Tinker, Tailor, Soldier, Spy\\" is very highly recommended to fans of LeCarre's excellent spy fiction.

It features a twisting storyline, an unblinking look at the moral ambiguity of the espionage business, and a haunting ending.<br /><br />\\"The Spy\\" is British agent Alec Leamas, recalled to London from Berlin after the loss of a valued agent.  There, the ruthless head of the British Secret Service offers him a chance to square accounts with the East Germans, by pretending to defect.<br /><br />To build Leamas the proper bonafides, he is cashiered from the service with an inadequate pension.  An alcoholic burn-out case, he is fired from a series of menial jobs, and even falls in love with the young secretary of a London communist cell, before ending up in prison.  When he is released, he is approached by the East Germans and persuaded to defect.<br /><br />In East Berlin, Leamas comes face to face with the senior East German intelligence officer he was meant to find.  He also discovers that a much deeper game is being played, in which betrayal can have many faces...<br /><br />\\"Spy\\" features several characters who would have recurring roles in later LeCarre novels, most notably George Smiley, Leamas' handler, himself a troubled soldier of the Cold War.<br /><br />\\"The Spy Who Came In From the Cold\\" is very highly recommended to fans of spy fiction, who are likely to find it one of the best in the genre.

B001N18HOQ|5.0|Pearl Jam's debut album, \\"Ten,\\" helped to upend the state of American Rock and Roll. Coming off the remnants of several other noteworthy bands (most of all, Mother Love Bone) and recruited San Diegian singer Eddie Vedder, \\"Ten\\" stripped the fluff and posturing away from late 80's hair-metal and scraped it down to primal guitar and deep angst. Vedder gave this perfect voice, and guitarist Mike McCready stayed more inside the classic rock manual when it came time for guitar leads.<br /><br />The result was an a album that resonated both the current rock lovers and the grunge crowd that was lusting for anything that gave of something that resembled Nirvana's grunge vibe. \\"Evenflow,\\" \\"Alive,\\" \\"Jeremy\\" and \\"Black\\" soon became established on radio and MTV, and the rest,as is often said, is history. That established, the most exciting thing about this deluxe remaster is the obvious care the band, producer Rick Parashar, bonus disc remixer Brendan O'Brien and other contributors put into this edition. The original album sounds as blistering as it always did, and the second disc rethinks the original while losing none of the impact. O'Brien reins in the echo/reverb, pushes Vedder more to the fore and makes things a bit cleaner. Disc one is like getting caught in a ravaging sandstorm, the remix is like getting a sand blaster applied in a precise location. Either way, you're getting pounded and laid bare.<br /><br />The DVD is the MTV Unplugged performance of March 1992 (roughly when \\"Ten\\" was beginning to peak), and captures the band in all its anti-swagger glory. The DVD is also devoid of clutter and scraps, making it a more valuable watch. The scraps are in the main packaging, with articles about Mookie Blaylock (Pearl Jam's original name), lots of pictures, posters, passes, and plenty of Vedder's surprisingly good sketches. The deluxe version of \\"Ten\\" is exactly how a re-issue should be done; no waste and plenty of value, respectful to both fan and band.

B0006Z2KYI|5.0|A brilliant, darkly funny film, Ernst Lubitsch's comedy about a Polish theatre troop and its efforts to thwart the Nazis was not well received in its day. The film was released in 1942, and a public in the midst of World War II may not have been in the mood to watch a film that made fun of what was, at the time, a real threat.<br /><br />While its initial reception was unfortunate, the film has since been rightly recognized as a classic. 1942 audiences apparently felt that much of the humor was in bad taste. You could argue, however, that trying to be tasteful would have been the wrong approach. Nothing about the Nazis or what they did could be considered good taste. And Lubitsch's humor was grounded in a genuine recognition of the seriousness of the Nazi threat. He mocks the banality of the Nazis, but he never forgets that they are, in fact, genuinely dangerous. The theatre troop (led by Carol Lombard and Jack Benny, both wonderful here) and its antics are farcical and often hilarious, but the filmmakers never forget the real danger that they're in. \\"To Be or Not to Be\\" is a fabulous piece of work.

In spite of the various calamities that befall the Stark clan, the first three books truly grabbed me and immersed me in Martin's world, to the point where I dutifully accepted the death and suffering of the main characters with the expectation and hope that this served the story. I thought I understood Arya's arch; she was to become the warrior/restorer of the house of Stark and Mr. Martin intended for the reader to experience her character's suffering and deconstruction in order to believe an  evolution from tomboyish maiden to cold killer/warrior princess. To make matters worse, her chapters are plodding and boring.  Mr. Martin's overindulging and frankly superfluous (to the overall game of thrones) description of Braavos adds further to the drudgery of Arya's chapters. To have to plod through Brianne's chapters only to endure the savage beating and subsequent hanging of this character is truly cruel and unusual punishment by Mr.Martin on his readers.     I am grateful that I didn't have to wait five years for Feast for Crowes.  I read, I endured, I'm confused and disappointed but also hopeful that DWD will set things right.     Why Mr. Martin decided to give us this meandering book instead of focusing on Jon Snow and Stannis, the red queen, Mance Rayder, Daenerys and Tyrion Lannister (and Varys) makes me think that he succumbed to the siren song of the almighty dollar.        This sadly reminds of the television series Lost and the precipitous decline in the story telling and aimless meandering of plot and characters as the producers tried to milk extra years out of a fantastic story.  They padded, profited,  and the series suffered greatly for it.      A Feast for Crows is padding of the Lost kind.

1416596585|5.0|If you are working in/with technology, buy and read 'In the Plex'. It will show you 'natural evolution' of a single strong idea blooming into a techno-bazaar. Just like evolution of life went through some extinctions, often inexplicable with available knowledge - Google's journey from infancy to teenage has also been rocked every now and then. Steven Levy masterfully crafts an captivating narrative that goes through 4P's of Google - People, Processes, Products and Protests against it. Deep insights about each of the P's are interspersed within an objective tale. What differentiates 'In The Plex' from earlier books (Googled; Search etc) is Mr. Levy's intense focus on people and characters. Software, like other intellectual media, is ultimately a reflection of creators' personality. e.g., the fact Google home page has minimal artifacts could perhaps be traced back to the founders' engineering core that strips away all unnecessary details and focuses on the solution. Many executives, especially engineers behind various products were profiled to show the consistency and imbuement of engineering-driven culture that the founders weaved around.    Some highlights from the book -    1) Before Google, search was mostly about 'throwing massive silicon toward the problem and hope for the best'.    2) Relevance is not a machine language attribute. It lies in the eye of beholder. Levy masterfully narrates the fiasco in searching Chinese language. As users started entering less number of key-strokes, the algorithm that worked so well in US faltered. The results produced were mostly risque.    3) Larry Page himself Okays each new employee before an offer is extended. Levy's book ends when Google had 24,000 employees. Larry Page wanted Google to have 1M employees.     4) Google usually asks for SAT scores from job applicants.    5) OKR - Objectives and Key Results is a key management tool in Google. It leads employees to set measurable and optimum targets. A bad example is 'I willimprove search in next quarter'. A good example would be 'I will improve average search page performance by 100 ms next quarter'. OKRs are cascaded from individual to team to department levels. The sweet spot is 0.7 to 0.8 of OKR target. Exceeding OKR may mean one has 'sandbagged' it.     6) Larry Page cares about latency at his core. Rather, he hates latency and always consciously tries to remove it 'like Lady Macbeth washing guilt off her hands'.  'If your product could be measured in seconds, you'd already failed'. Performance is a feature. Page's law dictates software slows down 2x every 18 months. Google's war room like 'Code Yellow' to tackle product performance should be a mandatory case study for all technology leaders.     A OKR for performance was measured in human lives. e.g., A human life is measured in 2B seconds. If a product has 100M users and is 4 sec slower for everyone each day, it is equivalent to 100 people killed a year! Now, try to live with that in your conscience if you are a developer ;-) To save the situation, Google has introduced 'Cap and Trade' for wasted performance. You can 'trade' 10 'lives' from another team that may have lived well within its target latency.    8) In data centers you can save about 20% just by raising the thermostat from 68 to 80 degrees. No issue with life here, unlike we just saw with bad performance.    9) At one point, one of the founders (guess who?) argued there should be no landlines in the company. The other one gave an idea to the author to release a chapter of this book at a time.    10) As of August 2010, there were about 130M different books published in the world in all languages.

B004EPZ084|4.0|There have been quite a few R-rated comedies this year, but they've all left something to be desired. Your Highness collapsed under being nothing but improvised toilet humor, The Hangover Part II was a complete rehash of the original, Hall Pass was just a disappointing attempt by the Farrelly brothers, and Bad Teacher was too tame to really be called \\"bad\\" at all. Paul and Bridesmaids were both pretty decent, but Paul wasn't nearly as good as the movies Simon Pegg and Nick Frost collaborated with Edgar Wright on and Bridesmaids pretty much played out like every other Judd Apatow movie out there. It was beginning to feel like every R-rated comedy from here on out would be so similar to everything before it that the laughs it tried to provide wouldn't be very funny at all. Thankfully, Horrible Bosses came along to prove that theory wrong.<br /><br />What's interesting is that Horrible Bosses kind of follows the same formula as The Hangover; three guys getting themselves into a really terrible situation but roofies are replaced with a crazy scheme to kill their bosses. The funniest aspect of the movie lies within three guys, Nick (Jason Bateman), Dale (Charlie Day), and Kurt (Jason Sudeikis), obviously having no idea what they're getting themselves into and dealing with the situation in the worst way imaginable. Things get really crazy really fast and only get more hectic as the movie jogs along. That's essentially the movie's charm. While the formulas to the two movies are similar, Horrible Bosses does seem to offer quite a bit more than The Hangover.<br /><br />The cast may be the strongest factor of the movie though as nearly everybody involved makes the most of their screen time and manages to get a laugh or two in no matter if they're a recognized name or a new face. Kevin Spacey, Colin Farrell, and Jennifer Aniston play the three bosses and they're all fantastic in their roles. Spacey just goes out of his way to be the most despicable person anyone could really come across while Farrell, despite being selfish and greedy, still manages to toss in a few pretty hysterical one-liners. Aniston will probably be the one that sticks out the most since she makes the biggest impact by being rather forward with her sexual desires for Dale. It's just such a different role from what Aniston has played in the past. The chemistry between Bateman, Day, and Sudeikis is really spot-on, as well. Their conversations, fighting amongst each other and interactions just make you really believe that these three have known each other for years. Even though his role is a little small, Jamie Foxx deserves a mention as well. Dean Jones turns out to just be a really entertaining character overall.<br /><br />It's difficult to point out any sort of problems this comedy really has, if there are any at all. The film is extremely vulgar, but doesn't really feel entirely too crude or include any gross out gags like many R-rated comedies seem to fall victim to. None of the cast drags the movie down as they all do their part to drive the movie forward. I feel like it won't be as successful as some would think though and will be overshadowed by other comedies that aren't nearly as solid or as funny as Horrible Bosses is. That's really the only complaint anyone could possibly have.<br /><br />Horrible Bosses is easily the most hilarious R-rated comedy of the summer. The strong cast, amusing dialogue, and entertaining banter between Nick, Dale, and Kurt is more than enough to sky rocket Horrible Bosses to the top of the list of comedies released this year. Charlie Day will have you laughing right from the start. Their conversations, fighting amongst each other and interactions just make you really believe that these three have known each other for years. Even though his role is a little small, Jamie Foxx deserves a mention as well. Dean Jones turns out to just be a really entertaining character overall.    It's difficult to point out any sort of problems this comedy really has, if there are any at all. The film is extremely vulgar, but doesn't really feel entirely too crude or include any gross out gags like many R-rated comedies seem to fall victim to. None of the cast drags the movie down as they all do their part to drive the movie forward. I feel like it won't be as successful as some would think though and will be overshadowed by other comedies that aren't nearly as solid or as funny as Horrible Bosses is.
